# National Federation of Independent Business v. Sebelius

 NATIONAL FEDERATION OF INDEPENDENT
 BUSINESS et al. v. SEBELIUS, SECRETARY
 OF HEALTH AND HUMAN
 SERVICES, et al.

certiorari to the united states court of appeals for
 the eleventh circuit

 No. 11вАУ 393. Argued March 26, 27, 28, 2012 вАФDecided June 28, 2012*
In 2010, Congress enacted the Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act
 in order to increase the number of Americans covered by health insur¬≠
 ance and decrease the cost of health care. One key provision is the
 individual mandate, which requires most Americans to maintain вАЬmini¬≠
 mum essentialвАЭ health insurance coverage. 26 U. S. C. ¬І 5000A. For
 individuals who are not exempt, and who do not receive health insurance
 through an employer or government program, the means of satisfying
 the requirement is to purchase insurance from a private company. Be¬≠
 g inning in 2014, those who do not comply with the mandate must make
 a вАЬ [s] h a r e d r e s p o n s i b i l i t y p a y m e n t вАЭ t o t h e Fe d e r a l G o v e r n m e n t .
 ¬І 5000A( b)(1). The Act provides that this вАЬpenaltyвАЭ will be paid
 to the Internal Revenue Service (IRS) with an individualвАЩs taxes, and
 вАЬshall be assessed and collected in the same manner вАЭ as tax penalties.
 ¬І¬І 5000A(c), (g)(1).
 Another key provision of the Act is the Medicaid expansion. The
 current Medicaid program offers federal funding to States to assist
 preg nant women, children, needy families, the blind, the elderly, and the
 disabled in obtaining medical care. 42 U. S. C. ¬І 1396d(a). The Afford¬≠
 able Care Act expands the scope of the Medicaid program and increases
 the number of individuals the States must cover. For example, the Act
 requires state programs to provide Medicaid coverage by 2014 to adults
 with incomes up to 133 percent of the federal poverty level, whereas
 many States now cover adults with children only if their income is con¬≠
 siderably lower, and do not cover childless adults at all. ¬І 1396a(a)(10)
 (A)( i)(VIII). The Act increases federal funding to cover the StatesвАЩ
 costs in expanding Medicaid coverage. ¬І 1396d(y)(1). But if a State
 does not comply with the ActвАЩs new coverage requirements, it may lose

 *Together with No. 11вАУ 398, Depar tmen t o f Heal th and Human Ser v ices
et al. v. Flor ida et al., and No. 11вАУ 400, Flor ida et al. v. Depar tmen t o f
Heal th and Human Ser v ices et al., also on certiorari to the same court.

520 NATIONAL FEDERATION OF INDEPENDENT
 BUSINESS v. SEBELIUS
 Syllabus

 not only the federal funding for those requirements, but all of its federal
 Medicaid funds. ¬І 1396c.
 Twenty-six States, several individuals, and the National Federation
 of Independent Business brought suit in Federal District Court, chal¬≠
 leng ing the constitutionality of the individual mandate and the Medicaid
 expansion. The Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit upheld the
 Medicaid expansion as a valid exercise of CongressвАЩs spending power,
 but concluded that Congress lacked authority to enact the individual
 mandate. Finding the mandate severable from the ActвАЩs other provi¬≠
 sions, the Eleventh Circuit lef t the rest of the Act intact.
Held: The judg ment is afпђБrmed in part and reversed in part.
648 F. 3d 1235, afпђБrmed in part and reversed in part.
 1. Chief Justice Roberts delivered the opinion of the Court with
 respect to Part II, concluding that the Anti-Injunction Act does not bar
 this suit.
 The Anti-Injunction Act provides that вАЬno suit for the purpose of re¬≠
 straining the assessment or collection of any tax shall be maintained in
 any court by any person, вАЭ 26 U. S. C. ¬І 7421(a), so that those subject to
 a tax must пђБrst pay it and then sue for a refund. The present challenge
 seeks to restrain the collection of the shared responsibility payment
 from those who do not comply with the individual mandate. But Con¬≠
 gress did not intend the payment to be treated as a вАЬ taxвАЭ for purposes
 of the Anti-Injunction Act. The Affordable Care Act describes the pay¬≠
 ment as a вАЬpenalty, вАЭ not a вАЬ tax. вАЭ That label cannot control whether
 the payment is a tax for purposes of the Constitution, but it does deter¬≠
 mine the application of the Anti-Injunction Act. The Anti-Injunction
 Act therefore does not bar this suit. Pp. 543 вАУ 546.
 2. Chief Justice Roberts concluded in Part IIIвАУA that the indi¬≠
 vidual mandate is not a valid exercise of CongressвАЩs power under the
 Commerce Clause and the Necessary and Proper Clause. Pp. 547вАУ 561.
 (a) The Constitution grants Congress the power to вАЬregula te Com¬≠
 merce. вАЭ Art. I, ¬І 8, cl. 3 (emphasis added). The power to regula te com¬≠
 merce presupposes the existence of commercial activity to be regulated.
 This CourtвАЩs precedent reпђВects this understanding: As expansive as this
 CourtвАЩs cases constr uing the scope of the commerce power have been,
 they uniformly describe the power as reaching вАЬactivity. вАЭ E. g., Un i ted
 Sta tes v. Lopez, 514 U. S. 549, 560. The individual mandate, however,
 does not regulate existing commercial activity. It instead compels indi¬≠
 viduals to beco me active in commerce by purchasing a product, on the
 ground that their failure to do so affects interstate commerce.
 Constr uing the Commerce Clause to permit Congress to regulate indi¬≠
 viduals precisely because they are doing nothing would open a new and

<a id="page-3"></a>
### Page 3

Cite as: 567 U. S. 519 (2012) 521

 Syllabus

potentially vast domain to congressional authority. Congress already
possesses expansive power to regulate what people do. Upholding the
Affordable Care Act under the Commerce Clause would g ive Congress
the same license to regulate what people do not do. The Framers knew
the difference between doing something and doing nothing. They gave
Congress the power to regula te commerce, not to co mpel it. Ig noring
that distinction would undermine the principle that the Federal Govern¬≠
ment is a government of limited and enumerated powers. The indi¬≠
vidual mandate thus cannot be sustained under CongressвАЩs power to
вАЬregulate Commerce. вАЭ Pp. 547вАУ 558.
 ( b) Nor can the individual mandate be sustained under the Neces¬≠
sary and Proper Clause as an integral part of the Affordable Care ActвАЩs
other reforms. Each of this CourtвАЩs prior cases upholding laws under
that Clause involved exercises of authority derivative of, and in service
to, a granted power. E. g., Un i ted Sta tes v. Co mstock, 560 U. S. 126.
The individual mandate, by contrast, vests Congress with the extraordi¬≠
nary ability to create the necessary predicate to the exercise of an enu¬≠
merated power and draw within its regulatory scope those who would
otherwise be outside of it. Even if the individual mandate is вАЬneces¬≠
saryвАЭ to the Affordable Care ActвАЩs other reforms, such an expansion of
federal power is not a вАЬproper вАЭ means for making those reforms effec¬≠
tive. Pp. 558 вАУ 561.
 3. Chief Justice Roberts concluded in Part IIIвАУB that the individ¬≠
ual mandate must be constr ued as imposing a tax on those who do not
have health insurance, if such a constr uction is reasonable.
 The most straightforward reading of the individual mandate is that
it commands individuals to purchase insurance. But, for the reasons
explained, the Commerce Clause does not g ive Congress that power.
It is therefore necessary to turn to the GovernmentвАЩs alternative argu¬≠
ment: that the mandate may be upheld as within CongressвАЩs power to
вАЬ lay and collect Taxes. вАЭ Art. I, ¬І 8, cl. 1. In pressing its taxing power
argument, the Government asks the Court to view the mandate as im¬≠
posing a tax on those who do not buy that product. Because вАЬevery
reasonable constr uction must be resorted to, in order to save a statute
from unconstitutionality, вАЭ Hooper v. Cal i for n i a, 155 U. S. 648, 657, the
question is whether it is вАЬfairly possibleвАЭ to interpret the mandate as
imposing such a tax, Crowell v. Benso n, 285 U. S. 22, 62. Pp. 561вАУ 563.
 4. Chief Justice Roberts delivered the opinion of the Court with
respect to Part IIIвАУ C, concluding that the individual mandate may
b e u p h e l d a s w i t h i n C o n g r e s s вАЩs p o w e r u n d e r t h e Ta x i n g C l a u s e .
Pp. 563 вАУ 574.
 (a) The Affordable Care Act describes the вАЬ [s]hared responsibility
paymentвАЭ as a вАЬpenalty, вАЭ not a вАЬ tax. вАЭ That label is fatal to the applica¬≠

<a id="page-4"></a>
### Page 4

522 NATIONAL FEDERATION OF INDEPENDENT
 BUSINESS v. SEBELIUS
 Syllabus

 tion of the Anti-Injunction Act. It does not, however, control whether
 an exaction is within CongressвАЩs power to tax. In answering that con¬≠
 stitutional question, this Court follows a functional approach, вАЬ [d]is¬≠
 regarding the desig nation of the exaction, and viewing its substance
 and application. вАЭ Un i ted Sta tes v. Co nstan t ine, 296 U. S. 287, 294.
 Pp. 563 вАУ 565.
 ( b) Such an analysis suggests that the shared responsibility pay¬≠
 ment may for constitutional purposes be considered a tax. The pay¬≠
 ment is not so high that there is really no choice but to buy health
 insurance; the payment is not limited to willful violations, as penalties
 for unlawful acts of ten are; and the payment is collected solely by the
 IRS through the normal means of taxation. Cf. Ch i ld L abor Tax Case
 (Bai ley v. D rexel Fur n i ture Co.), 259 U. S. 20, 36 вАУ 37. None of this is
 to say that payment is not intended to induce the purchase of health
 insurance. But the mandate need not be read to declare that failing to
 do so is unlawful. Neither the Affordable Care Act nor any other law
 attaches negative legal consequences to not buying health insurance,
 beyond requiring a payment to the IRS. And CongressвАЩs choice of lan¬≠
 guageвАФstating that individuals вАЬshallвАЭ obtain insurance or pay a вАЬpen-
 altyвАЭвАФ does not require reading ¬І 5000A as punishing unlawful conduct.
 It may also be read as imposing a tax on those who go without
 insurance. See New York v. Un i ted Sta tes, 505 U. S. 144, 169 вАУ174.
 Pp. 565 вАУ 570.
 (c) Even if the mandate may reasonably be characterized as a tax,
 it must still comply with the Direct Tax Clause, which provides: вАЬNo
 Capitation, or other direct, Tax shall be laid, unless in Proportion to the
 Census or Enumeration herein before directed to be taken. вАЭ Art. I, ¬І 9,
 cl. 4. A tax on going without health insurance is not like a capitation or
 other direct tax under this CourtвАЩs precedents. It therefore need not
 be apportioned so that each State pays in proportion to its population.
 Pp. 570 вАУ 571.
 5. Chief Justice Roberts, joined by Justice Breyer and Justice
 Kagan, concluded in Part IV that the Medicaid expansion violates the
 Constitution by threatening States with the loss of their existing Medic¬≠
 aid funding if they decline to comply with the expansion. Pp. 575 вАУ 588.
 (a) The Spending Clause grants Congress the power вАЬ to pay the
 Debts and provide for the . . . general Welfare of the United States. вАЭ
 Art. I, ¬І 8, cl. 1. Congress may use this power to establish cooperative
 state-federal Spending Clause programs. The leg itimacy of Spend¬≠
 ing Clause leg islation, however, depends on whether a State voluntarily
 and knowingly accepts the terms of such programs. Pennhurst Sta te
 Schoo l and Hospi tal v. Halder man, 451 U. S. 1, 17. вАЬ [T]he Constitution
 simply does not g ive Congress the authority to require the States to

<a id="page-5"></a>
### Page 5

Cite as: 567 U. S. 519 (2012) 523

 Syllabus

regulate. вАЭ New York, supra, at 178. When Congress threatens to ter¬≠
minate other grants as a means of pressuring the States to accept a
Spending Clause program, the leg islation r uns counter to this NationвАЩs
system of federalism. Cf. Sou th Dako ta v. Do le, 483 U. S. 203, 211.
Pp. 575 вАУ 581.
 ( b) Section 1396c g ives the Secretary of Health and Human Serv¬≠
ices the authority to penalize States that choose not to participate in
the Medicaid expansion by taking away their existing Medicaid funding.
42 U. S. C. ¬І 1396c. The threatened loss of over 10 percent of a StateвАЩs
overall budget is economic dragooning that leaves the States with no
real option but to acquiesce in the Medicaid expansion. The Govern¬≠
ment claims that the expansion is properly viewed as only a modiпђБcation
of the existing program, and that this modiпђБcation is permissible be¬≠
cause Congress reserved the вАЬright to alter, amend, or repeal any provi¬≠
sionвАЭ of Medicaid. ¬І 1304. But the expansion accomplishes a shif t in
kind, not merely degree. The orig inal program was desig ned to cover
medical services for particular categories of vulnerable individuals.
Under the Affordable Care Act, Medicaid is transformed into a program
to meet the health care needs of the entire nonelderly population with
income below 133 percent of the poverty level. A State could hardly
anticipate that CongressвАЩs reservation of the right to вАЬalter вАЭ or вАЬamendвАЭ
the Medicaid program included the power to transform it so dramati¬≠
cally. The Medicaid expansion thus violates the Constitution by threat¬≠
ening States with the loss of their existing Medicaid funding if they
decline to comply with the expansion. Pp. 581вАУ 585.
 (c) The constitutional violation is fully remedied by precluding the
Secretary from applying ¬І 1396c to withdraw existing Medicaid funds
for failure to comply with the requirements set out in the expansion.
See ¬І 1303. The other provisions of the Affordable Care Act are not
affected. Congress would have wanted the rest of the Act to stand,
had it known that States would have a genuine choice whether to partic¬≠
ipate in the Medicaid expansion. Pp. 585 вАУ 588.
 6. Justice Ginsburg, joined by Justice Sotomayor, is of the view
that the Spending Clause does not preclude the Secretary from with¬≠
holding Medicaid funds based on a StateвАЩs refusal to comply with the
expanded Medicaid program. But g iven the majority view, she agrees
with The Chief JusticeвАЩs conclusion in Part IVвАУB that the Medicaid
ActвАЩs severability clause, 42 U. S. C. ¬І 1303, determines the appropriate
remedy. Because The Chief Justice пђБnds the withholdingвАФnot the
grantingвАФof federal funds incompatible with the Spending Clause, Con¬≠
gressвАЩ extension of Medicaid remains available to any State that af¬≠
пђБrms its willing ness to participate. Even absent ¬І 1303вАЩs command, the
Court would have no warrant to invalidate the funding offered by the

<a id="page-6"></a>
### Page 6

 Medicaid expansion, and surely no basis to tear down the Affordable
 Care Act in its entirety. When a court confronts an unconstitutional
 statute, its endeavor must be to conserve, not destroy, the leg islation.
 See, e. g., Ayo tte v. Planned Paren thood o f Nor ther n New Eng., 546
 U. S. 320, 328 вАУ 330. Pp. 645 вАУ 646.

spect to Part IV, in which Breyer and Kagan, JJ., joined; and an opinion

 Today, the restr icti ons on gover nment power foremost in
many Amer icansвАЩ minds are likely to be afпђБr mative proh ibi¬≠
ti ons, such as contained in the Bill of Rights. These afпђБr m¬≠
ative proh ibiti ons come into play, however, only where the
Gover nment possesses author ity to act in the пђБrst place. If
no enumerated power author izes Congress to pass a cer tain
law, that law may not be enacted, even if it wou ld not vi olate
any of the express proh ibiti ons in the Bill of Rights or else¬≠
where in the Constituti on.
 Indeed, the Constituti on did not initia lly include a Bill of
Rights at least par tly because the Framers felt the enumera¬≠
ti on of powers sufпђБced to restrain the Gover nment. As Al¬≠
exander Hamilton put it, вАЬ the Constituti on is itself, in every
rati ona l sense, and to every usefu l purpose, a bill of
rights. вАЭ The Federa list No. 84, p. 515 (C. Rossiter ed.
1961). And when the Bill of Rights was ratiпђБed, it made
express what the enumerati on of powers necessar ily implied:
вАЬ The powers not delegated to the United States by the Con¬≠
stituti on . . . are reserved to the States respectively, or to
the people. вАЭ U. S. Const., Amdt. 10. The Federa l Gover n¬≠
ment has expanded dramatica lly over the past two centur ies,
but it still must show that a constituti ona l grant of power
author izes each of its acti ons. See, e. g., Un i ted Sta tes v.
Co m st ock, 560 U. S. 126 (2010).
 The same does not apply to the States, because the Consti¬≠
tuti on is not the source of their power. The Constituti on
may restr ict state gover nmentsвАФas it does, for example, by
forbidding them to deny any person the equa l protecti on of
the laws. But where such proh ibiti ons do not apply, state
gover nments do not need constituti ona l author izati on to act.
The States thus can and do perfor m many of the vita l func¬≠
ti ons of moder n gover nment вАФpunish ing street cr ime, r un¬≠
ning public schools, and zoning proper ty for development, to
name but a fewвАФ even though the Constituti onвАЩs text does

<a id="page-18"></a>
### Page 18

536 NATIONAL FEDERATION OF INDEPENDENT

 Opinion of Roberts, C. J.

not author ize any gover nment to do so. Our cases refer to
th is genera l power of gover ning, possessed by the States
but not by the Federa l Gover nment, as the вАЬpolice power. вАЭ
See, e. g., Un i ted Sta tes v. M o r r iso n, 529 U. S. 598, 618 вАУ 619
(2000).
 вАЬState sovereig nty is not just an end in itself : Rather, fed¬≠

diffusi on of sovereig n power. вАЭ New Yo rk v. Un i ted Sta tes,
505 U. S. 144, 181 (1992) ( inter na l quotati on marks omitted).
Because the police power is controlled by 50 different States
instead of one nati ona l sovereig n, the facets of gover ning
that touch on citizensвАЩ daily lives are nor ma lly administered
by sma ller gover nments closer to the gover ned. The Fram¬≠

Bo nd v. Un i ted Sta tes, 564 U. S. 211, 222 (2011).
 Th is case concer ns two powers that the Constituti on does

fu lly to avoid creating a genera l federa l author ity ak in to
the police power. The Constituti on author izes Congress to
вАЬreg u late Commerce w ith foreig n Nati ons, and among the

Our precedents read that to mean that Congress may reg¬≠

that substantia lly affect interstate commerce. вАЭ M o r r iso n,
s u p r a , a t 6 0 9 ( i n t e r n a l q u o t a t i o n m a r k s o m i t t e d ) . T h e
power over activities that substantia lly affect interstate
commerce can be expansive. That power has been held to

author ize federa l reg u lati on of such seeming ly loca l matters

w ith the letter and spir it of the constituti on, are constitu¬≠

 Our per missive reading of these powers is explained in
par t by a genera l reticence to inva lidate the acts of the Na¬≠

ti onвАЩs elected leaders. вАЬProper respect for a co-ordinate

to pass [the] act in questi on is clear ly demonstrated. вАЭ
Un i ted Sta tes v. Har r is, 106 U. S. 629, 635 (1883). Members

our Nati onвАЩs elected leaders, who can be thrown out of ofпђБce

John Marsha ll, A Fr iend of the Constituti on No. V, Alexan¬≠

Cull och v. Mar y l and 190 вАУ191 (G. Gunther ed. 1969). And
there can be no questi on that it is the responsibility of th is
Cour t to enforce the limits on federa l power by str ik ing
down acts of Congress that transgress those limits. Mar-
b ur y v. Mad iso n, supra, at 175 вАУ176.
 The questi ons before us must be considered against the

 II
 Before tur ning to the mer its, we need to be sure we have
the author ity to do so. The Anti-In juncti on Act provides
that вАЬno suit for the purpose of restraining the assessment
or collecti on of any tax sha ll be maintained in any cour t by
any person, whether or not such person is the person against
whom such tax was assessed. вАЭ 26 U. S. C. ¬І 7421(a). Th is
statute protects the Gover nmentвАЩs ability to collect a con¬≠
sistent stream of revenue, by barr ing litigati on to en join or
other w ise obstr uct the collecti on of taxes. Because of the
Anti-In juncti on Act, taxes can ordinar ily be cha llenged only
af ter they are paid, by suing for a refund. See Enochs v.
Wi ll i am s Packing & Nav. Co., 370 U. S. 1, 7вАУ 8 (1962).
 The pena lty for not comply ing w ith the Affordable Care
ActвАЩs individua l mandate пђБrst becomes enforceable in 2014.
The present cha llenge to the mandate thus seeks to restrain
the pena ltyвАЩs future collecti on. Amicus contends that the
Inter na l Revenue Code treats the pena lty as a tax, and that
the Anti-In juncti on Act therefore bars th is suit.
 The text of the per tinent statutes suggests other w ise.
The Anti-In juncti on Act applies to suits вАЬfor the purpose
of restraining the assessment or collecti on of any tax. вАЭ
¬І 7421(a) (emphasis added). Congress, however, chose to de¬≠
scr ibe the вАЬ [s]hared responsibility paymentвАЭ imposed on
those who forgo hea lth insurance not as a вАЬ tax, вАЭ but as a
вАЬpena lty. вАЭ ¬І¬І 5000A( b), (g)(2). There is no immediate rea¬≠
son to th ink that a statute apply ing to вАЬany taxвАЭ wou ld apply
to a вАЬpena lty. вАЭ

 2 We appointed H. Bartow Farr III to brief and argue in support of the

 CongressвАЩs decisi on to label th is exacti on a вАЬpena ltyвАЭ
rather than a вАЬ taxвАЭ is sig niпђБcant because the Affordable Care
Act descr ibes many other exacti ons it creates as вАЬ taxes. вАЭ
See Tho mas M o re, 651 F. 3d, at 551. Where Congress uses
cer tain lang uage in one par t of a statute and different lan¬≠
g uage in another, it is genera lly presumed that Congress
acts intenti ona lly. See Russell o v. Un i ted Sta tes, 464 U. S.
16, 23 (1983).
 Amicus arg ues that even though Congress did not label
the shared responsibility payment a tax, we shou ld treat it
as such under the Anti-In juncti on Act because it functi ons
like a tax. It is tr ue that Congress cannot change whether
an exacti on is a tax or a pena lty for co n st i tu t io n al purposes
simply by descr ibing it as one or the other. Congress may
not, for example, expand its power under the Taxing Clause,
or escape the Double Jeopardy ClauseвАЩs constraint on cr imi¬≠
na l sancti ons, by labeling a severe пђБnancia l punishment a
вАЬ tax. вАЭ See Ch i ld L abo r Tax Case (Bai ley v. D rexel Fur n i¬≠
ture Co.), 259 U. S. 20, 36 вАУ 37 (1922); Depar tmen t o f Rev enue
o f M o n t. v. Kur th Ranch, 511 U. S. 767, 779 (1994).
 The Anti-In juncti on Act and the Affordable Care Act,
however, are creatures of CongressвАЩs own creati on. How
they relate to each other is up to Congress, and the best
evidence of CongressвАЩs intent is the statutory text. We have
thus applied the Anti-In juncti on Act to statutor ily descr ibed
вАЬ taxesвАЭ even where that label was inaccurate. See Bai ley
v. Geo rge, 259 U. S. 16 (1922) (Anti-In juncti on Act applies to
вАЬCh ild Labor TaxвАЭ str uck down as exceeding CongressвАЩs tax¬≠
ing power in D rexel Fur n i ture).
 Congress can, of course, descr ibe someth ing as a pena lty
but direct that it nonetheless be treated as a tax for pur¬≠
poses of the Anti-In juncti on Act. For example, 26 U. S. C.
¬І 6671(a) provides that вАЬany reference in th is title to вАШ taxвАЩ
imposed by th is title sha ll be deemed a lso to refer to the
pena lties and liabilities provided byвАЭ Subchapter 68B of the
Inter na l Revenue Code. Pena lties in Subchapter 68B are
thus treated as taxes under Title 26, wh ich includes the Anti¬≠

<a id="page-27"></a>
### Page 27

Cite as: 567 U. S. 519 (2012) 545

 Opinion of the Court

In juncti on Act. The individua l mandate, however, is not in
Subchapter 68B of the Code. Nor does any other provi¬≠
si on state that references to taxes in Title 26 sha ll a lso be
вАЬdeemedвАЭ to apply to the individua l mandate.
 Amicus attempts to show that Congress did render the
Anti-In juncti on Act applicable to the individua l mandate, a l¬≠
beit by a more circuitous route. Secti on 5000A(g)(1) speci¬≠
пђБes that the pena lty for not comply ing w ith the mandate
вАЬsha ll be assessed and collected in the same manner as an
assessable pena lty under subchapter B of chapter 68. вАЭ As¬≠
sessable pena lties in Subchapter 68B, in tur n, вАЬsha ll be as¬≠
s e s s e d a n d c o l l e c t e d i n t h e s a m e m a n n e r a s t a x e s . вАЭ
¬І 6671(a). According to amicus, by directing that the pen¬≠
a lty be вАЬassessed and collected in the same manner as taxes, вАЭ
¬І 5000A(g)(1) made the Anti-In juncti on Act applicable to
th is pena lty.
 The Gover nment disagrees. It arg ues that ¬І 5000A(g)(1)
does not direct cour ts to apply the Anti-In juncti on Act, be¬≠
cause ¬І 5000A(g) is a directive only to the Secretary of the
Treasury to use the same вАЬ вАШmethodolog y and proceduresвАЩ вАЭ
to collect the pena lty that he uses to collect taxes. Br ief for
United States 32 вАУ 33 (quoting Sev en-Sky, 661 F. 3d, at 11).
 We th ink the Gover nment has the better reading. As it
observes, вАЬAssessmentвАЭ and вАЬCollecti onвАЭ are chapters of the
Inter na l Revenue Code providing the Secretary author ity to

wheat by suppor ting its pr ice. But pr ice can be suppor ted

No. 48, at 309 (J. Madison). Congress a lready en joys vast

 To an economist, perhaps, there is no difference between

f e c t s o n c o m m e r c e . B u t t h e d i s t i n c t i o n b e t w e e n d o i n g

вАЬ the framers of the Constituti on were not mere visi onar ies,
toy ing w ith specu lati ons or theor ies, but practica l men, dea l¬≠

199 U. S. 437, 449 (1905). The Framers gave Congress the
power to regul a te commerce, not to co mpel it, and for over
200 years both our decisi ons and CongressвАЩs acti ons have re¬≠

from that understanding now.
 The Gover nment sees th ings differently. It arg ues that
because sickness and in jury are unpredictable but unavoid¬≠

hea lth care, wh ich they reg u lar ly seek and obtain. вАЭ Br ief

But вАЬself-insuranceвАЭ is, in this context, nothing more than a description of

562 NATIONAL FEDERATION OF INDEPENDENT

ment, the Gover nment defended the mandate as a reg u lati on

Gover nment does not claim that the taxing power a llows

 The text of a statute can sometimes have more than one
possible meaning. To take a familiar example, a law that
reads вАЬno veh icles in the park вАЭ might, or might not, ban bicy¬≠
cles in the park. And it is well established that if a statute
has two possible meanings, one of wh ich vi olates the Consti¬≠

Justice Story said that 180 years ago: вАЬNo cour t ought, unless

intenti ona l, of the constituti on. вАЭ Parso n s v. Bed fo rd, 3 Pet.
433, 448вАУ 449 (1830). Justice Holmes made the same point a
century later: вАЬ [T]he r u le is settled that as between two pos¬≠
sible interpretati ons of a statute, by one of wh ich it wou ld
be unconstituti ona l and by the other va lid, our plain duty is
to adopt that wh ich w ill save the Act. вАЭ Bl od ge t t v. Ho lden,
275 U. S. 142, 148 (1927) (concurr ing opini on).
 The most straightfor ward reading of the mandate is that
it commands individua ls to purchase insurance. Af ter a ll, it
states that individua ls вАЬsha llвАЭ maintain hea lth insurance. 26
U. S. C. ¬І 5000A(a). Congress thought it cou ld enact such a

hea lth insurance, the only consequence is that he must make

### Page 45

Cite as: 567 U. S. 519 (2012) 563

 Opinion of the Court

an additi ona l payment to the IRS when he pays h is taxes.
See ¬І 5000A( b). That, according to the Gover nment, means

As we have explained, вАЬevery reasonable constr ucti on must

564 NATIONAL FEDERATION OF INDEPENDENT

must assess and collect it вАЬ in the same manner as taxes. вАЭ

applicati on of the Anti-In juncti on Act, supra, at 544 вАУ 545, it

 Our precedent reпђВects th is: In 1922, we decided two cha l¬≠

the Anti-In juncti on Act to apply. In the second case, how¬≠

D rexel Fur n i ture, 259 U. S., at 38. That constituti ona l
questi on was not controlled by CongressвАЩs choice of label.
 We have similar ly held that exacti ons not labeled taxes
nonetheless were author ized by CongressвАЩs power to tax. In
the Licen se Tax Cases, for example, we held that federa l
licenses to sell liquor and lottery ticketsвАФfor wh ich the li¬≠

<a id="page-47"></a>
### Page 47

Cite as: 567 U. S. 519 (2012) 565

 Opinion of the Court

censee had to pay a feeвАФcou ld be sustained as exercises of

exacti on only on those who know ing ly employed underage

 Even when the Direct Tax Clause was wr itten it was un¬≠
clear what else, other than a capitati on (a lso known as a
вАЬ head taxвАЭ or a вАЬpoll taxвАЭ), might be a direct tax. See
Spr inge r v. Un i ted Sta tes, 102 U. S. 586, 596 вАУ 598 (1881).
Soon af ter the framing, Congress passed a tax on ownersh ip
of carr iages, over James MadisonвАЩs objecti on that it was an

v. Un i ted Sta tes, 3 Da ll. 171, 174 (1796) (opini on of Chase, J.).

The Cour t was unanimous, and those Justices who wrote
opini ons either directly asser ted or strong ly suggested that
only two for ms of taxati on were direct: capitati ons and land
taxes. See i d., at 175; i d., at 177 (opini on of Paterson, J.);
i d., at 183 (opini on of Iredell, J.).
 That narrow view of what a direct tax might be persisted
for a century. In 1880, for example, we explained that вАЬd i¬≠
rect taxes, w ith in the meaning of the Constituti on, are only
capitati on taxes, as expressed in that instr ument, and taxes
on rea l estate. вАЭ Spr inge r, supra, at 602. In 1895, we ex¬≠
panded our interpretati on to include taxes on persona l prop¬≠
er ty and income from persona l proper ty, in the course of
str ik ing down aspects of the federa l income tax. Po ll ock v.
Far me rsвАЩ Lo an & Tr ust Co., 158 U. S. 601, 618 (1895). That
resu lt was over tur ned by the Sixteenth Amendment, a l¬≠
though we continued to consider taxes on persona l proper ty
to be direct taxes. See Eisne r v. Maco mbe r, 252 U. S. 189,
218 вАУ 219 (1920).
 A tax on going w ithout hea lth insurance does not fa ll
w ith in any recog nized category of direct tax. It is not a
capitati on. Capitati ons are taxes paid by every person,
вАЬw ithout regard to proper ty, professi on, or any o the r cir¬≠
cum stance. вАЭ Hy l t o n, supra, at 175 (opini on of Chase, J.)
(emphasis a ltered). The whole point of the shared responsi¬≠
bility payment is that it is tr iggered by speciпђБc circum¬≠
stancesвАФ ear ning a cer tain amount of income but not obtain¬≠
ing hea lth insurance. The payment is a lso plainly not a tax
on the ownersh ip of land or persona l proper ty. The shared
responsibility payment is thus not a direct tax that must be
appor ti oned among the severa l States.
 There may, however, be a more fundamenta l objecti on to
a tax on those who lack hea lth insurance. Even if only a
tax, the payment under ¬І 5000A( b) remains a burden that the
Federa l Gover nment imposes for an omissi on, not an act. If
it is troubling to interpret the Commerce Clause as author iz¬≠
ing Congress to reg u late those who abstain from commerce,

perhaps it shou ld be similar ly troubling to per mit Congress
to impose a tax for not doing someth ing.
 Three considerati ons a llay th is concer n. First, and most
impor tantly, it is abundantly clear the Constituti on does not
g uarantee that individua ls may avoid taxati on through inac¬≠
tivity. A capitati on, af ter a ll, is a tax that everyone must
pay simply for existing, and capitati ons are expressly con¬≠
templated by the Constituti on. The Cour t today holds that
our Constituti on protects us from federa l reg u lati on under
the Commerce Clause so long as we abstain from the reg u¬≠
lated activity. But from its creati on, the Constituti on has
made no such promise w ith respect to taxes. See Letter
from Ben jamin Franklin to M. Le Roy (Nov. 13, 1789), in 10
Works of Ben jamin Franklin 410 (1944) (вАЬOur new Constitu¬≠
ti on is now established . . . but in th is wor ld noth ing can be
said to be cer tain, except death and taxesвАЭ).
 Whether the mandate can be upheld under the Commerce
Clause is a questi on about the scope of federa l author ity.
Its answer depends on whether Congress can exercise what
a ll acknowledge to be the novel course of directing individ¬≠
ua ls to purchase insurance. CongressвАЩs use of the Taxing
Clause to encourage buy ing someth ing is, by contrast, not
new. Tax incentives a lready promote, for example, purchas¬≠
i n g h o m e s a n d p r o f e s s i o n a l e d u c a t i o n s . S e e 2 6 U. S . C.
¬І¬І 163( h), 25A. Sustaining the mandate as a tax depends
only on whether Congress has proper ly exercised its taxing
power to encourage purchasing hea lth insurance, not whether
it can. Upholding the individua l mandate under the Tax¬≠
ing Clause thus does not recog nize any new federa l power.
It deter mines that Congress has used an existing one.
 Second, CongressвАЩs ability to use its taxing power to in¬≠
пђВuence conduct is not w ithout limits. A few of our cases
policed these limits aggressively, inva lidating punitive ex¬≠
acti ons obvi ously desig ned to reg u late behavi or other w ise
regarded at the time as beyond federa l author ity. See, e. g.,
Un i ted Sta tes v. Bu tle r, 297 U. S. 1 (1936); D rexel Fur n i ture,

<a id="page-55"></a>
### Page 55

Cite as: 567 U. S. 519 (2012) 573

 Opinion of the Court

259 U. S. 20. More of ten and more recently we have de¬≠
clined to closely examine the reg u latory motive or effect of
revenue-raising measures. See Kahr i ge r, 345 U. S., at 27вАУ
31 (collecting cases). We have nonetheless maintained that
вАЬ вАШ there comes a time in the extensi on of the pena lizing fea¬≠
tures of the so-ca lled tax when it loses its character as such
and becomes a mere pena lty w ith the character istics of reg u¬≠
lati on and punishment. вАЩ вАЭ Kur th Ranch, 511 U. S., at 779
(quoting D rexel Fur n i ture, supra, at 38).
 We have a lready explained that the shared responsibility
paymentвАЩs practica l character istics pass muster as a tax
under our narrowest interpretati ons of the taxing power.
Supra, at 567вАУ 568. Because the tax at hand is w ith in even
those str ict limits, we need not here decide the precise point
at wh ich an exacti on becomes so punitive that the taxing
power does not author ize it. It remains tr ue, however,
that the вАЬ вАШpower to tax is not the power to destroy wh ile
th is Cour t sits. вАЩ вАЭ Ok l aho ma Tax Co mmвАЩn v. Texas Co.,
336 U. S. 342, 364 (1949) (quoting Panhand le O i l Co. v. M is¬≠
sissipp i ex rel. Knox, 277 U. S. 218, 223 (1928) (Holmes, J.,
dissenting)).
 Th ird, a lthough the breadth of CongressвАЩs power to tax is
greater than its power to reg u late commerce, the taxing
power does not g ive Congress the same degree of control
over individua l behavi or. Once we recog nize that Congress
may reg u late a par ticu lar decisi on under the Commerce
Clause, the Federa l Gover nment can br ing its fu ll weight to
bear. Congress may simply command individua ls to do as it
directs. An individua l who disobeys may be subjected to
cr imina l sancti ons. Those sancti ons can include not only
пђБnes and impr isonment, but a ll the attendant consequences
of being branded a cr imina l: depr ivati on of other w ise pro¬≠
tected civil r ights, such as the r ight to bear ar ms or vote in
electi ons; loss of employment oppor tunities; socia l stig ma;
and severe disabilities in other controversies, such as custody
or immigrati on disputes.

 By contrast, CongressвАЩs author ity under the taxing power
is limited to requir ing an individua l to pay money into the
Federa l Treasury, no more. If a tax is proper ly paid, the
Gover nment has no power to compel or punish individua ls
subject to it. We do not make light of the severe burden
that taxati onвАФ especia lly taxati on motivated by a reg u latory
purposeвАФcan impose. But impositi on of a tax nonetheless
leaves an individua l w ith a lawfu l choice to do or not do a
cer tain act, so long as he is w illing to pay a tax levied on
that choice.11
 The Affordable Care ActвАЩs requirement that cer tain indi¬≠
vidua ls pay a пђБnancia l pena lty for not obtaining hea lth insur¬≠
ance may reasonably be character ized as a tax. Because the
Constituti on per mits such a tax, it is not our role to forbid
it, or to pass upon its w isdom or fair ness.

 D
 Justice Ginsburg questi ons the necessity of re jecting
the Gover nmentвАЩs commerce power arg ument, g iven that
¬І 5000A can be upheld under the taxing power. Post, at 623.
But the statute reads more natura lly as a command to buy
insurance than as a tax, and I wou ld uphold it as a command
if the Constituti on a llowed it. It is only because the Com¬≠
merce Clause does not author ize such a command that it is
necessary to reach the taxing power questi on. And it is
only because we have a duty to constr ue a statute to save it,
if fair ly possible, that ¬І 5000A can be interpreted as a tax.

and may sometimes face prosecution for failing to do so (although not

¬І 5000A(g)(2)). But that does not show that the tax restricts the lawful
choice whether to undertake or forgo the activity on which the tax is
predicated. Those subject to the individual mandate may lawfully forgo

Without deciding the Commerce Clause questi on, I wou ld

 The Federa l Gover nment does not have the power to order
people to buy hea lth insurance. Secti on 5000A wou ld there¬≠
fore be unconstituti ona l if read as a command. The Federa l
Gover nment does have the power to impose a tax on those
w ithout hea lth insurance. Secti on 5000A is therefore consti¬≠

 The Spending Clause grants Congress the power вАЬ to pay

federa l funds to the States, and may conditi on such a grant
upon the StatesвАЩ вАЬ tak ing cer tain acti ons that Congress cou ld
not require them to take. вАЭ Co llege Sav ings Bank, 527 U. S.,
at 686. Such measures вАЬencourage a State to reg u late in a
par ticu lar way, [and] inпђВuenc[e] a StateвАЩs policy choices. вАЭ
New Yo rk, supra, at 166. The conditi ons imposed by Con¬≠
gress ensure that the funds are used by the States to вАЬpro¬≠

intended.
 At the same time, our cases have recog nized limits on Con¬≠
gressвАЩs power under the Spending Clause to secure state
compliance w ith federa l objectives. вАЬWe have repeatedly

<a id="page-59"></a>
### Page 59

Cite as: 567 U. S. 519 (2012) 577

 Opinion of Roberts, C. J.

character ized . . . Spending Clause leg islati on as вАШmuch in the
nature of a co n tract. вАЩ вАЭ Bar nes v. Go r man, 536 U. S. 181,

ter ms of the вАШcontract. вАЩ вАЭ Id., at 17. Respecting th is limita¬≠

does not under mine the status of the States as independent
sovereig ns in our federa l system. That system вАЬrests on
what might at пђБrst seem a counter intuitive insight, that
вАШfreedom is enhanced by the creati on of two gover nments,
not one. вАЩ вАЭ Bo nd, 564 U. S., at 220 вАУ 221 (quoting Alden v.
Maine, 527 U. S. 706, 758 (1999)). For th is reason, вАЬ the Con¬≠

the ability to require the States to gover n according to Con¬≠

the two-gover nment system established by the Framers
wou ld g ive way to a system that vests power in one centra l
gover nment, and individua l liber ty wou ld suffer.
 That insight has led th is Cour t to str ike down federa l leg¬≠

вАЬpower ak in to undue inпђВuence. вАЭ Stew ard Mach ine Co. v.
Dav is, 301 U. S. 548, 590 (1937). Congress may use its
spending power to create incentives for States to act in ac¬≠
cordance w ith federa l policies. But when вАЬpressure tur ns
into compu lsi on, вАЭ ib i d., the leg islati on r uns contrary to our

<a id="page-60"></a>
### Page 60

system of federa lism. вАЬ [T]he Constituti on simply does not

### Page 61

Cite as: 567 U. S. 519 (2012) 579

 Opinion of Roberts, C. J.

that Congress adopted the cha llenged tax and abatement

 B
 Noth ing in our opini on precludes Congress from offer ing

 That fu lly remedies the constituti ona l vi olati on we have
identiпђБed. The chapter of the United States Code that con¬≠

we need go no fur ther. That clause speciпђБes that вАЬ [i]f any

TodayвАЩs holding does not affect the continued applicati on of
¬І 1396c to the existing Medicaid program. Nor does it affect
the SecretaryвАЩs ability to w ithdraw funds provided under the
Affordable Care Act if a State that has chosen to par ticipate

that Act.
 Th is is not to say, as the joint dissent suggests, that we
are вАЬrewr iting the Medicaid Expansi on. вАЭ Post, at 691. In¬≠
stead, we deter mine, пђБrst, that ¬І 1396c is unconstituti ona l

cumstances. вАЭ ¬І 1303. When we inva lidate an applicati on
of a statute because that applicati on is unconstituti ona l, we
are not вАЬrewr iting вАЭ the statute; we are merely enforcing
the Constituti on.
 The questi on remains whether todayвАЩs holding affects
other provisi ons of the Affordable Care Act. In consider ing
that questi on, вАЬ [w]e seek to deter mine what Congress wou ld
have intended in light of the Cour tвАЩs constituti ona l holding. вАЭ
Un i ted Sta tes v. Booke r, 543 U. S. 220, 246 (2005) ( inter na l
quotati on marks omitted). Our вАЬ touchstone for any decisi on
about remedy is leg islative intent, for a cour t cannot use its
remedia l powers to circumvent the intent of the leg islature. вАЭ
Ayo t te v. Pl anned Paren thood o f No r the r n New Eng., 546
U. S. 320, 330 (2006) ( inter na l quotati on marks omitted).

<a id="page-69"></a>
### Page 69

Cite as: 567 U. S. 519 (2012) 587

 Opinion of Roberts, C. J.

The questi on here is whether Congress wou ld have wanted
the rest of the Act to stand, had it known that States wou ld
have a genuine choice whether to par ticipate in the new
Medicaid expansi on. Unless it is вАЬevidentвАЭ that the answer
is no, we must leave the rest of the Act intact. Champl in
Re пђБn ing Co. v. Co r po ra t io n Co mmвАЩn o f Ok l a., 286 U. S. 210,
234 (1932).
 We are conпђБdent that Congress wou ld have wanted to pre¬≠
serve the rest of the Act. It is fair to say that Congress
assumed that every State wou ld par ticipate in the Medicaid
expansi on, g iven that States had no rea l choice but to do so.
The States contend that Congress enacted the rest of the
Act w ith such fu ll par ticipati on in mind; they point out that
Congress made Medicaid a means for satisfy ing the mandate,
26 U. S. C. ¬І 5000A(f )(1)(A)( i i), and enacted no other plan for
providing coverage to many low-income individua ls. Accord¬≠
ing to the States, th is means that the entire Act must fa ll.
 We disagree. The Cour t today limits the пђБnancia l pres¬≠
sure the Secretary may apply to induce States to accept the
ter ms of the Medicaid expansi on. As a practica l matter,
that means States may now choose to re ject the expansi on;
that is the whole point. But that does not mean a ll or even
any w ill. Some States may indeed decline to par ticipate,
either because they are unsure they w ill be able to afford
their share of the new funding obligati ons, or because they
are unw illing to commit the administrative resources neces¬≠
sary to suppor t the expansi on. Other States, however, may
voluntar ily sig n up, пђБnding the idea of expanding Medicaid
coverage attractive, par ticu lar ly g iven the level of federa l
funding the Act offers at the outset.
 We have no way of know ing how many States w ill accept
the ter ms of the expansi on, but we do not believe Congress
wou ld have wanted the whole Act to fa ll, simply because
some may choose not to par ticipate. The other refor ms
Congress enacted, af ter a ll, w ill remain вАЬfu lly operative as a
law, вАЭ Champl in, supra, at 234, and w ill still functi on in a

<a id="page-70"></a>
### Page 70

588 NATIONAL FEDERATION OF INDEPENDENT
 BUSINESS v. SEBELIUS
 Opinion of Roberts, C. J.

way вАЬconsistent w ith CongressвАЩ basic objectives in enacting
the statute, вАЭ Booke r, supra, at 259. ConпђБdent that Con¬≠
gress wou ld not have intended anyth ing different, we con¬≠
clude that the rest of the Act need not fa ll in light of our
constituti ona l holding.

 * * *
 The Affordable Care Act is constituti ona l in par t and un¬≠
constituti ona l in par t. The individua l mandate cannot be
upheld as an exercise of CongressвАЩs power under the Com¬≠
merce Clause. That Clause author izes Congress to reg u late
interstate commerce, not to order individua ls to engage in
it. In th is case, however, it is reasonable to constr ue what
Congress has done as increasing taxes on those who have a
cer tain amount of income, but choose to go w ithout hea lth
i n s u r a n c e. S u c h l e g i s l a t i o n i s w i t h i n C o n g r e s s вАЩs p o w e r
to tax.
 As for the Medicaid expansi on, that por ti on of the Afford¬≠
able Care Act vi olates the Constituti on by threatening exist¬≠
ing Medicaid funding. Congress has no author ity to order
the States to reg u late according to its instr ucti ons. Con¬≠
gress may offer the States grants and require the States to
comply w ith accompany ing conditi ons, but the States must
have a genuine choice whether to accept the offer. The
States are g iven no such choice in th is case: They must either
accept a basic change in the nature of Medicaid, or r isk losing
a ll Medicaid funding. The remedy for that constituti ona l vi¬≠
olati on is to preclude the Federa l Gover nment from imposing
such a sancti on. That remedy does not require str ik ing
down other por ti ons of the Affordable Care Act.
 The Framers created a Federa l Gover nment of limited
powers, and assig ned to th is Cour t the duty of enforcing
those limits. The Cour t does so today. But the Cour t does
not express any opini on on the w isdom of the Affordable
Care Act. Under the Constituti on, that judg ment is re¬≠
served to the people.

problems directly and rea listica lly. вАЭ Ame r ican Po w e r &

 Until today, th is Cour tвАЩs prag matic approach to judg ing

When appraising such leg islati on, we ask only (1) whether

 Nor is it accurate to say that the minimum coverage pro¬≠

 III
 A
 For the reasons explained above, the minimum coverage
provisi on is va lid Commerce Clause leg islati on. See Par t
II, supra. When viewed as a component of the entire ACA,
the provisi onвАЩs constituti ona lity becomes even plainer.
 The Necessary and Proper Clause вАЬempowers Congress to
enact laws in effectuati on of its [commerce] powe[r] that are
not w ith in its author ity to enact in isolati on. вАЭ Raich, 545
U. S., at 39 (Scalia, J., concurr ing in judg ment). Hence, вАЬ [a]
complex reg u latory program . . . can survive a Commerce
Clause cha llenge w ithout a show ing that every sing le facet
of the program is independently and directly related to a
va lid congressi ona l goa l. вАЭ Ind i an a, 452 U. S., at 329, n. 17.
вАЬIt is enough that the cha llenged provisi ons are an integra l
par t of the reg u latory program and that the reg u latory
scheme when considered as a whole satisпђБes th is test. вАЭ
Ib i d. (collecting cases). See a lso Raich, 545 U. S., at 24 вАУ 25
(A cha llenged statutory provisi on пђБts w ith in CongressвАЩ com¬≠

w ith them in the implementati on of federa l programs.

statutory war ning that Congress may change the require¬≠

 Justice Scalia, Justice Kennedy, Justice Thomas,

 Congress has set out to remedy the problem that the best
hea lth care is beyond the reach of many Amer icans who can¬≠
not afford it. It can assuredly do that, by exercising the
powers accorded to it under the Constituti on. The questi on
in th is case, however, is whether the complex str uctures and

<a id="page-129"></a>
### Page 129

Cite as: 567 U. S. 519 (2012) 647

 Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ., dissenting

provisi ons of the Patient Protecti on and Affordable Care Act
(Affordable Care Act, Act, or ACA) go beyond those powers.
We conclude that they do.
 Th is case is in one respect difпђБcu lt: It presents two ques¬≠
ti ons of пђБrst impressi on. The пђБrst of those is whether fail¬≠
ure to engage in economic activity (the purchase of hea lth
insurance) is subject to reg u lati on under the Commerce
Clause. Failure to act does resu lt in an effect on commerce,
and hence might be said to come under th is Cour tвАЩs вАЬaffecting
commerceвАЭ cr iter i on of Commerce Clause jur ispr udence.
But in none of its decisi ons has th is Cour t extended the
Clause that far. The second questi on is whether the con¬≠
gressi ona l power to tax and spend, U. S. Const., Ar t. I, ¬І 8,
cl. 1, per mits the conditi oning of a StateвАЩs continued receipt
of a ll funds under a massive state-administered federa l wel¬≠
fare program upon its acceptance of an expansi on to that
program. Severa l of our opini ons have suggested that the
power to tax and spend cannot be used to coerce state admin¬≠
istrati on of a federa l program, but we have never found a law
enacted under the spending power to be coercive. Those
questi ons are difпђБcu lt.
 The case is easy and straightfor ward, however, in another
respect. What is absolutely clear, afпђБr med by the text of
the 1789 Constituti on, by the Tenth Amendment ratiпђБed in
1791, and by innumerable cases of ours in the 220 years since,
is that there are str uctura l limits upon federa l powerвАФupon
what it can prescr ibe w ith respect to pr ivate conduct, and
upon what it can impose upon the sovereig n States. What¬≠
ever may be the conceptua l limits upon the Commerce
Clause and upon the power to tax and spend, they cannot be
such as w ill enable the Federa l Gover nment to reg u late a ll
pr ivate conduct and to compel the States to functi on as ad¬≠
ministrators of federa l programs.
 That clear pr inciple carr ies the day here. The str ik ing
case of Wickard v. Fi lb ur n, 317 U. S. 111 (1942), wh ich held
that the economic activity of grow ing wheat, even for oneвАЩs

<a id="page-130"></a>
### Page 130

648 NATIONAL FEDERATION OF INDEPENDENT
 BUSINESS v. SEBELIUS
 Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ., dissenting

o w n c o n s u m p t i o n , a f f e c t e d c o m m e r c e s u f f i c i e n t l y t h a t i t
cou ld be reg u lated, a lways has been regarded as the ne plus
ul tra of expansive Commerce Clause jur ispr udence. To go
beyond that, and to say the fai lure to grow wheat (wh ich is
no t an economic activity, or any activity at a ll) nonetheless
affects commerce and therefore can be federa lly reg u lated,
is to make mere breath ing in and out the basis for federa l
prescr ipti on and to extend federa l power to vir tua lly a ll
human activity.
 As for the constituti ona l power to tax and spend for the
genera l welfare: The Cour t has long since expanded that be¬≠
yond (what Madison thought it meant) taxing and spending
for those aspects of the genera l welfare that were w ith in
the Federa l Gover nmentвАЩs enumerated powers, see Un i ted
Sta tes v. Bu tle r, 297 U. S. 1, 65 вАУ 66 (1936). Thus, we now
have sizable federa l Depar tments devoted to subjects not
menti oned among CongressвАЩ enumerated powers, and only

them. In our view it must follow that the entire statute

 C
 A few respectfu l responses to Justice Ginsburg вАЩs dissent
on the issue of the Mandate are in order. That dissent du ly
recites the test of Commerce Clause power that our opini ons
have applied, but disregards the premise the test contains.
It is tr ue enough that Congress needs only a вАЬ вАШrati ona l basisвАЩ
for concluding that the regul a ted act i v i ty substantia lly af¬≠

<a id="page-140"></a>
### Page 140

658 NATIONAL FEDERATION OF INDEPENDENT
 BUSINESS v. SEBELIUS
 Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ., dissenting

fects interstate commerce, вАЭ an te, at 602 (emphasis added).
But it must be act i v i ty affecting commerce that is reg u lated,
and not merely the failure to engage in commerce. And one
is not now purchasing the hea lth care covered by the insur¬≠
ance mandate simply because one is likely to be purchasing
it in the future. Our testвАЩs premise of reg u lated activity is
not invented out of whole cloth, but rests upon the Constitu¬≠
ti onвАЩs requirement that it be commerce wh ich is reg u lated.
If a ll inactivity affecting commerce is commerce, commerce
is everyth ing. Ultimately the dissent is dr iven to say ing
that there is rea lly no difference between acti on and inacti on,
an te, at 612 вАУ 613, a propositi on that has never recommended
itself, neither to the law nor to common sense. To say, for
example, that the inacti on here consists of activity in вАЬ the
self-insurance market, вАЭ an te, at 613, seems to us wordplay.
By par ity of reasoning the failure to buy a car can be ca lled
par ticipati on in the non-pr ivate-car-transpor tati on market.
Commerce becomes everyth ing.
 The dissent claims that we вАЬfai[ l] to explain why the indi¬≠
vidua l mandate threatens our constituti ona l order. вАЭ An te,
at 621. But we have done so. It threatens that order be¬≠
cause it g ives such an expansive meaning to the Commerce
Clause that all pr ivate conduct ( including failure to act)
becomes subject to federa l control, effectively destroy ing
the Constituti onвАЩs divisi on of gover nmenta l powers. Thus
the dissent, on the theor ies proposed for the va lidity of
the Mandate, wou ld a lter the accepted constituti ona l rela¬≠
ti on between the individua l and the Nati ona l Gover nment.
The dissent protests that the Necessary and Proper Clause
has been held to include вАЬ the power to enact cr imina l

 II
 The Tax i n g Power
 As far as ¬І 5000A is concer ned, we wou ld stop there. Con¬≠
gress has attempted to reg u late beyond the scope of its Com¬≠

The Gover nment contends, however, as expressed in the cap¬≠

provision is independently authorized by congressвАЩs

The phrase вАЬ independently author izedвАЭ suggests the exist¬≠
ence of a creature never h ither to seen in the United States
Repor ts: a pena lty for constituti ona l purposes that is also a
tax for constituti ona l purposes. In a ll our cases the two are
mutua lly exclusive. The provisi on cha llenged under the
Constituti on is either a pena lty or else a tax. Of course in
many cases what was a reg u latory mandate enforced by a
pena lty co uld hav e been imposed as a tax upon per missible
acti on; or what was imposed as a tax upon per missible acti on
co uld hav e been a reg u latory mandate enforced by a pena lty.
But we know of no case, and the Gover nment cites none, in
wh ich the impositi on was, for constituti ona l purposes, both.5
The two are mutua lly exclusive. Thus, what the Gover n¬≠
mentвАЩs capti on shou ld have read was вАЬalternatively, the
minimum coverage provision is not a mandate-with¬≠
penalty but a tax.вАЭ It is impor tant to bear th is in mind
in eva luating the tax arg ument of the Gover nment and of

 5 Of course it can be both for statutory purposes, since Congress can
deпђБne вАЬ taxвАЭ and вАЬpenaltyвАЭ in its enactments any way it wishes. That is
why Un i ted Sta tes v. So telo, 436 U. S. 268 (1978), does not disprove our

 Our cases establish a clear line between a tax and a pen¬≠

suppor t of gover nment; a pena lty . . . is an exacti on imposed

Sta tes v. Reo rgan i zed CF&I Fabr ica t o rs o f U tah, Inc., 518

held that a вАЬ taxвАЭ imposed upon pr ivate conduct was so oner¬≠

nev e rвАФthat a pena lty imposed for vi olati on of the law was
so tr ivia l as to be in effect a tax. We have never held that
any exacti on imposed for vi olati on of the law is an exercise
of CongressвАЩ taxing powerвАФ even when the statute calls it

ment suggests a pena ltyвАФthough one can imag ine a tax
imposed only on w illfu l acti on; but the absence of such a re¬≠
quirement does not suggest a tax. Pena lties for absolute-
liability offenses are commonplace. And where a statute is
silent as to scienter, we traditi ona lly presume a men s rea
requirement if the statute imposes a вАЬsevere pena lty. вАЭ Sta¬≠
ples v. Un i ted Sta tes, 511 U. S. 600, 618 (1994). Since we
have an entire jur ispr udence addressing when it is that a
scienter requirement shou ld be inferred from a pena lty, it is
quite illog ica l to suggest that a pena lty is not a pena lty for
want of an express scienter requirement.
 And the nail in the cofпђБn is that the mandate and pena lty
are located in Title I of the Act, its operative core, rather
than where a tax wou ld be foundвАФin Title IX, containing
the ActвАЩs вАЬRevenue Provisi ons. вАЭ In sum, вАЬ the ter ms of [the]
act rende[r] it unavoidable, вАЭ Parso n s v. Bed fo rd, 3 Pet. 433,
448 (1830), that Congress imposed a reg u latory pena lty, not
a tax.
 For a ll these reasons, to say that the Individua l Mandate
merely imposes a tax is not to interpret the statute but to
rewr ite it. Judicia l tax-wr iting is par ticu lar ly troubling.
Taxes have never been popu lar, see, e. g., Stamp Act of 1765,
and in par t for that reason, the Constituti on requires tax
increases to or ig inate in the House of Representatives. See
Ar t. I, ¬І 7, cl. 1. That is to say, they must or ig inate in the
leg islative body most accountable to the people, where leg is¬≠
lators must weigh the need for the tax against the terr ible
pr ice they might pay at their next electi on, wh ich is never
more than two years off. The Federa list No. 58 вАЬdefend[ed]
the decisi on to g ive the or ig inati on power to the House on
the ground that the Chamber that is more accountable to
the people shou ld have the pr imary role in raising revenue. вАЭ
Un i ted Sta tes v. Munoz-Fl o res, 495 U. S. 385, 395 (1990).
We have no doubt that Congress knew precisely what it was

### Page 151

Cite as: 567 U. S. 519 (2012) 669

 Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ., dissenting

doing when it re jected an ear lier versi on of th is leg islati on
that imposed a tax instead of a requirement-w ith-pena lty.
See Affordable Hea lth Care for Amer ica Act, H. R. 3962,

Ib i d.

lic, and it w ill be federa l ofпђБcia ls that suffer the consequences
if the decisi on tur ns out to be detr imenta l or unpopu lar. вАЭ
New Yo rk, 505 U. S., at 168. But when the Federa l Gover n¬≠
ment compels the States to take unpopu lar acti ons, вАЬ it may
be state ofпђБcia ls who w ill bear the br unt of public disap¬≠
prova l, wh ile the federa l ofпђБcia ls who devised the reg u latory
program may remain insu lated from the electora l ramiпђБca¬≠
ti ons of their decisi on. вАЭ Id., at 169; see Pr in tz, 521 U. S.,
at 930. For th is reason, federa l ofпђБceholders may view th is
вАЬdepar tur[e] from the federa l str ucture to be in their per¬≠
sona l interests . . . as a means of sh if ting responsibility for
the eventua l decisi on. вАЭ New Yo rk, 505 U. S., at 182 вАУ183.
And even state ofпђБcia ls may favor such a вАЬdepar ture from
the constituti ona l plan, вАЭ since uncer tainty concer ning re¬≠
sponsibility may a lso per mit them to escape accountability.
Id., at 182. If a program is popu lar, state ofпђБcia ls may claim
credit; if it is unpopu lar, they may protest that they were
merely responding to a federa l directive.
 Once it is recog nized that spending-power leg islati on can¬≠
not coerce state par ticipati on, two questi ons remain: (1)
What is the meaning of coerci on in th is context? (2) Is the
ACAвАЩs expanded Medicaid coverage coercive? We now tur n
to those questi ons.
 D
 1
 The answer to the пђБrst of these questi onsвАФthe meaning of
coerci on in the present context вАФis straightfor ward. As we
have explained, the leg itimacy of attach ing conditi ons to fed¬≠
era l grants to the States depends on the voluntar iness of
the StatesвАЩ choice to accept or decline the offered package.

<a id="page-161"></a>
### Page 161

Cite as: 567 U. S. 519 (2012) 679

 Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ., dissenting

Therefore, if States rea lly have no choice other than to ac¬≠
cept the package, the offer is coercive, and the conditi ons
cannot be sustained under the spending power. And as our
decisi on in So u th Dako ta v. Do le makes clear, theoretica l
voluntar iness is not enough.
 In So u th Dako ta v. Do le, we considered whether the
spending power per mitted Congress to conditi on 5% of the
StateвАЩs federa l h ighway funds on the StateвАЩs adopti on of a
minimum dr ink ing age of 21 years. South Dakota arg ued
that the program was imper missibly coercive, but we dis¬≠
agreed, reasoning that вАЬCongress ha[d] directed only that a
State desir ing to establish a minimum dr ink ing age lower
than 21 lose a relatively sma ll percentage of cer tain federa l
h ighway funds. вАЭ 483 U. S., at 211. Because вАЬa ll South Da¬≠
kota wou ld lose if she adhere[d] to her chosen course as to a
suitable minimum dr ink ing age [was] 5% of the funds other¬≠
w ise obtainable under speciпђБed h ighway grant programs, вАЭ
we found that вАЬCongress ha[d] offered relatively mild encour¬≠
agement to the States to enact h igher minimum dr ink ing
ages than they wou ld other w ise choose. вАЭ Ib i d. Thus, the
decisi on whether to comply w ith the federa l conditi on вАЬre¬≠
main[ed] the prerogative of the States no t me rely in theo r y
b u t in fact, вАЭ and so the program at issue did not exceed Con¬≠
gressвАЩ power. Id., at 211вАУ 212 (emphasis added).
 The questi on whether a law enacted under the spending
power is coercive in fact w ill sometimes be difпђБcu lt, but
where Congress has plainly вАЬcrossed the line disting uish ing
encouragement from coerci on, вАЭ New Yo rk, supra, at 175, a
federa l program that coopts the StatesвАЩ politica l processes
must be declared unconstituti ona l. вАЬ [T]he federa l ba lance is
too essentia l a par t of our constituti ona l str ucture and plays
too vita l a role in secur ing freedom for us to admit inabil¬≠
ity to intervene. вАЭ Lo pez, 514 U. S., at 578 (Kennedy, J.,
concurr ing).

pays lipservice to the anticoerci on pr inciple. The Federa l

era l Gover nment w ill change funding ter ms and reduce the
percentage of funds it w ill cover. Th is wou ld leave the
States to bear an increasing ly large percentage of the bill.
See Tr. of Ora l Arg. in No. 11вАУ 400, pp. 74 вАУ76 (Mar. 28, 2012).
Fina lly, af ter 2015, the States w ill have to pick up the tab

 Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ., dissenting

for 50% of a ll administrative costs associated w ith imple¬≠

 In sum, it is perfectly clear from the goa l and str ucture of

that Congress understood no State cou ld refuse. The Med¬≠

 F
 Seven Members of the Cour t agree that the Medicaid
Expansi on, as enacted by Congress, is unconstituti ona l. See
Par ts IVвАУA to IVвАУE, supra; Par t IVвАУA, an te, at 575 вАУ 585
(opini on of Roberts, C. J., joined by Breyer and Kagan,
JJ.). Because the Medicaid Expansi on is unconstituti ona l,
the questi on of remedy ar ises. The most natura l remedy
wou ld be to inva lidate the Medicaid Expansi on. However,
the Gover nment proposesвАФin two cursory sentences at the
very end of its br iefвАФpreserving the Expansi on. Under its
proposa l, States wou ld receive the additi ona l Medicaid funds
if they expand elig ibility, but States wou ld keep their pre¬≠
existing Medicaid funds if they do not expand elig ibility.
We cannot accept the Gover nmentвАЩs suggesti on.
 The rea lity that States were g iven no rea l choice but to
expand Medicaid was not an accident. Congress assumed
States wou ld have no choice, and the ACA depends on
StatesвАЩ having no choice, because its Mandate requires low-
income individua ls to obtain insurance many of them can af¬≠
ford only through the Medicaid Expansi on. Fur ther more, a
StateвАЩs w ithdrawa l might subject everyone in the State to
much h igher insurance premiums. That is because the Med¬≠
icaid Expansi on w ill no longer offset the cost to the insurance

industry imposed by the ACAвАЩs insurance reg u lati ons and

 Worse, the Gover nmentвАЩs proposed remedy introduces a

 The Gover nment cites a severability clause codiпђБed w ith

sent no more than x percent of the StateвАЩs budget. The

 We shou ld not accept the Gover nmentвАЩs invitati on to at¬≠

 V
 Severab i l it y
 The Affordable Care Act seeks to ach ieve вАЬnear-universa lвАЭ
hea lth insurance coverage. ¬І 18091(2)(D) (2006 ed., Supp.
IV). The two pillars of the Act are the Individua l Mandate
and the expansi on of coverage under Medicaid. In our view,
both these centra l provisi ons of the Act вАФthe Individua l
Mandate and Medicaid Expansi onвАФare inva lid. It follows,
as some of the par ties urge, that a ll other provisi ons of the
Act must fa ll as well. The follow ing secti on explains the
severability pr inciples that require th is conclusi on. Th is
ana lysis a lso shows how closely interrelated the Act is, and
th is is a ll the more reason why it is judicia l usurpati on to
impose an entirely new mechanism for w ithdrawa l of Med¬≠
icaid funding, see Par t IVвАУF, supra, wh ich is one of many
examples of how rewr iting the Act a lters its dynamics.
 A
 When an unconstituti ona l provisi on is but a par t of a more
comprehensive statute, the questi on ar ises as to the va lidity
of the remaining provisi ons. The Cour tвАЩs author ity to de¬≠
clare a statute par tia lly unconstituti ona l has been well estab¬≠
lished since Marb ur y v. Mad iso n, 1 Cranch 137 (1803), when
the Cour t severed an unconstituti ona l provisi on from the Ju¬≠

<a id="page-174"></a>
### Page 174

692 NATIONAL FEDERATION OF INDEPENDENT
 BUSINESS v. SEBELIUS
 Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ., dissenting

diciary Act of 1789. And wh ile the Cour t has sometimes
applied вАЬat least a modest presumpti on in favor of . . . sever-
ability, вАЭ C. Nelson, Statutory Interpretati on 144 (2011), it
has not a lways done so, see, e. g., M inneso ta v. M i lle L acs
Band o f Ch ippew a Ind i an s, 526 U. S. 172, 190 вАУ195 (1999).
 An automatic or too cursory severance of statutory provi¬≠
si ons r isks вАЬrewr it[ing] a statute and g iv[ing] it an effect
a ltogether different from that sought by the measure viewed
as a whole. вАЭ Rai lro ad Re t iremen t Bd. v. Al t o n R. Co., 295
U. S. 330, 362 (1935). The Judiciary, if it orders uncr itica l
severance, then assumes the leg islative functi on; for it im¬≠
poses on the Nati on, by the Cour tвАЩs decree, its own new stat¬≠
utory reg ime, consisting of policies, r isks, and duties that
Congress did not enact. That can be a more extreme exer¬≠
cise of the judicia l power than str ik ing the whole statute and
a llow ing Congress to address the conditi ons that per tained
when the statute was considered at the outset.
 The Cour t has applied a two-par t g uide as the framework
for severability ana lysis. The test has been deemed вАЬwell
established. вАЭ Al aska Airl ines, Inc. v. Brock, 480 U. S. 678,
684 (1987). First, if the Cour t holds a statutory provisi on
unconstituti ona l, it then deter mines whether the now tr un¬≠
cated statute w ill operate in the manner Congress intended.
If not, the remaining provisi ons must be inva lidated. See
i d., at 685. In Al aska Airl ines, the Cour t clar iпђБed that th is
пђБrst inquiry requires more than ask ing whether вАЬ the ba lance
of the leg islati on is incapable of functi oning independently. вАЭ
Id., at 684. Even if the remaining provisi ons w ill operate in
some coherent way, that a lone does not save the statute.
The questi on is whether the provisi ons w ill work as Con¬≠
gress intended. The вАЬrelevant inquiry in eva luating sever-
ability is whether the statute w ill functi on in a manne r con¬≠
sistent w ith the intent of Congress. вАЭ Id., at 685 (emphasis
in or ig ina l). See a lso Free En te r pr ise Fund v. Publ ic Co m¬≠
pany Acco un t ing O v e rsi gh t Bd., 561 U. S. 477, 509 (2010)
(the Act вАЬremains fu lly operative as a law w ith these tenure

<a id="page-175"></a>
### Page 175

Cite as: 567 U. S. 519 (2012) 693

 Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ., dissenting

r e s t r i c t i o n s e x c i s e d вАЭ ( i n t e r n a l q u o t a t i o n m ar ks om it te d) );
Un i ted Sta tes v. Booke r, 543 U. S. 220, 227 (2005) (вАЬ [T]wo
provisi ons . . . must be inva lidated in order to a llow the stat¬≠
ute to operate in a manner consistent w ith congressi ona l in¬≠
tentвАЭ); M i lle L acs, supra, at 194 (вАЬ [ E]mbody ing as it did one

 Second, even if the remaining provisi ons can operate as
Congress desig ned them to operate, the Cour t must deter¬≠
mine if Congress wou ld have enacted them standing a lone
a n d w i t h o u t t h e u n c o n s t i t u t i o n a l p o r t i o n . I f C o n g r e s s
wou ld not, those provisi ons, too, must be inva lidated. See
Al aska Airl ines, supra, at 685 (вАЬ [T]he unconstituti ona l pro¬≠

sence is leg islati on that Congress wou ld not have enactedвАЭ);
see a lso Free En te r pr ise Fund, supra, at 509 (вАЬ [N]oth ing
in the statuteвАЩs text or h istor ica l context makes it вАШevi¬≠
dentвАЩ that Congress, faced w ith the limitati ons imposed by

to a Board whose members are removable at w illвАЭ); Ayo t te
v. Pl anned Paren thood o f No r the r n New Eng., 546 U. S.
320, 330 (2006) (вАЬWou ld the leg islature have preferred what
is lef t of its statute to no statute at a llвАЭ); Denv e r Area Ed.
Teleco mmun ica t io n s Co n so r t ium, Inc. v. FCC, 518 U. S.
727, 767 (1996) (plura lity opini on) (вАЬWou ld Congress still
have passed ¬І 10(a) had it known that the remaining provi¬≠
si ons were inva lidвАЭ ( inter na l quotati on marks and brackets
omitted)).
 The two inquir iesвАФwhether the remaining provisi ons w ill
operate as Congress desig ned them, and whether Congress
w o u l d h a v e e n a c t e d t h e r e m a i n i n g p r o v i s i o n s s t a n d i n g
a loneвАФof ten are interrelated. In the ordinary course, if the
remaining provisi ons cannot operate according to the con¬≠
gressi ona l desig n (the пђБrst inquiry), it a lmost necessar ily fol¬≠
lows that Congress wou ld not have enacted them (the second
inquiry). Th is close interacti on may explain why the Cour t
has not a lways been precise in disting uish ing between the

two. There are, however, occasi ons in wh ich the severabil¬≠

proxy for the second inquiry (whether the Leg islature in¬≠

 B
 The Act was passed to enable affordable, вАЬnear-universa lвАЭ
hea lth insurance coverage. 42 U. S. C. ¬І 18091(2)(D). The

 Congress did not intend to establish the goa l of near-

See, e. g., ACA ¬І 1563, 124 Stat. 270 (вАЬ [T]h is Act w ill reduce
the Federa l deпђБcit between 2010 and 2019вАЭ). And it did not

par ties. Thus, individua ls are required to obtain hea lth in¬≠

and Educati on Reconciliati on Act of 2010 (HCERA) ¬І 1401,

 C
 One preliminary point shou ld be noted before apply ing
severability pr inciples to the Act. To be sure, an arg ument
can be made that those por ti ons of the Act that none of the

 Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ., dissenting

par ties has standing to cha llenge cannot be held nonsever¬≠

like the ACA. It wou ld take years, perhaps decades, for

some of them (those simply expending federa l funds) no one

 The opini on now explains in Par t VвАУ CвАУ1, in f ra, why the
ActвАЩs major provisi ons are not severable from the Mandate
and Medicaid Expansi on. It proceeds from the insurance

in f ra, explains why the ActвАЩs minor provisi ons a lso are not
severable.
 1
 The ActвАЩs Ma jor P rov i sion s

reducti ons, the exchanges and their federa l subsidies, and

That resu lt follows from the undoubted inability of the other
major provisi ons to operate as Congress intended w ithout

 The Cour t has been infor med by disting uished economists

 The ActвАЩs desig n is to a llocate billi ons of federa l dollars to

 The ActвАЩs M i nor P rov i sion s
 The next questi on is whether the inva lidati on of the ACAвАЩs

 The ACA is over 900 pages long. Its reg u lati ons include

then it doesnвАЩt speak well of them. ThatвАЩs what th is leg isla¬≠
ti on is a ll about: ItвАЩs the ar t of compromise. вАЩ вАЭ Pear, In
Hea lth Bill for Everyone, Provisi ons for a Few, N. Y. Times,
Jan. 4, 2010, p. A10 (quoting Sen. Reid). Of ten, a minor pro¬≠
visi on w ill be the pr ice paid for suppor t of a major provisi on.
So, if the major provisi on were unconstituti ona l, Congress
wou ld not have passed the minor one.

 Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ., dissenting

 Without the ACAвАЩs major provisi ons, many of these minor

For example, the tax increases are вАЬRevenue Offset Pro¬≠

 Some provisi ons, such as requir ing chain restaurants to
display nutr iti ona l content, appear likely to operate as Con¬≠
gress intended, but they fail the second test for severability.
There is no reason to believe that Congress wou ld have
enacted them independently. The Cour t has not previ ously
had occasi on to consider severability in the context of an om¬≠
nibus enactment like the ACA, wh ich includes not only many
provisi ons that are ancillary to its centra l provisi ons but a lso
many that are entirely unrelatedвАФh itched on because it was
a quick way to get them passed despite oppositi on, or be¬≠

qu i d pro quo for their needed suppor t. When we are con¬≠
fronted w ith such a so-ca lled вАЬChr istmas tree, вАЭ a law to
wh ich many nonger mane or naments have been attached, we
th ink the proper r u le must be that when the tree no longer
exists the or naments are superпђВuous. We have no reliable
basis for know ing wh ich pieces of the Act wou ld have passed
on their own. It is cer tain that many of them wou ld not
have, and it is not a proper functi on of th is Cour t to g uess
wh ich. To sever the statute in that manner вАЬ вАШwou ld be to
make a new law, not to enforce an old one. Th is is not par t
of our duty. вАЩ вАЭ Trade-Mark Cases, 100 U. S., at 99.
 Th is Cour t must not impose r isks unintended by Congress
or produce leg islati on Congress may have lacked the suppor t

<a id="page-188"></a>

to enact. For those reasons, the unconstituti ona lity of both

the inva lidati on of the Affordable Care ActвАЩs other provisi ons.

 * * *
 The Cour t today decides to save a statute Congress did
not wr ite. It r u les that what the statute declares to be a
requirement w ith a pena lty is instead an opti on subject to a
tax. And it changes the intenti ona lly coercive sancti on of a
tota l cutoff of Medicaid funds to a supposedly noncoercive
cutoff of only the incrementa l funds that the Act makes
available.
 The Cour t regards its strained statutory interpretati on as
judicia l modesty. It is not. It amounts instead to a vast
judicia l overreach ing. It creates a debilitated, inoperable
versi on of hea lth care reg u lati on that Congress did not enact
and the public does not expect. It makes enactment of sen¬≠
sible hea lth care reg u lati on more difпђБcu lt, since Congress
cannot star t afresh but must take as its point of depar ture a
jumble of now senseless provisi ons, provisi ons that cer tain
interests favored under the Cour tвАЩs new desig n w ill str ugg le
to retain. And it leaves the public and the States to expend
vast sums of money on requirements that may or may not
survive the necessary congressi ona l revisi on.
 The Cour tвАЩs dispositi on, invented and atextua l as it is, does
not even have the mer it of avoiding constituti ona l difпђБcu lties.
It creates them. The holding that the Individua l Mandate
is a tax raises a difпђБcu lt constituti ona l questi on (what is a
direct tax?) that the Cour t resolves w ith inadequate deliber¬≠
ati on. And the judg ment on the Medicaid Expansi on issue
ushers in new federa lism concer ns and places an unaccus¬≠
tomed strain upon the Uni on. Those States that decline
the Medicaid Expansi on must subsidize, by the federa l tax
dollars taken from their citizens, vast grants to the States
that accept the Medicaid Expansi on. If that destabilizing
politica l dynamic, so antagonistic to a har moni ous Uni on, is

<a id="page-189"></a>
### Page 189

Cite as: 567 U. S. 519 (2012) 707

 Thomas, J., dissenting

to be introduced at a ll, it shou ld be by Congress, not by the
Judiciary.
 The va lues that shou ld have deter mined our course today
are cauti on, minima lism, and the understanding that the
Federa l Gover nment is one of limited powers. But the
Cour tвАЩs r u ling under mines those va lues at every tur n. In
the name of restraint, it overreaches. In the name of consti¬≠
tuti ona l avoidance, it creates new constituti ona l questi ons.
In the name of cooperative federa lism, it under mines state
sovereig nty.
 The Constituti on, though it dates from the founding of the
Republic, has powerfu l meaning and vita l relevance to our
own times. The constituti ona l protecti ons that th is case
involves are protecti ons of str ucture. Str uctura l protec¬≠
ti onsвАФnotably, the restraints imposed by federa lism and
separati on of powersвАФare less romantic and have less obvi¬≠

the Bill of Rights or the Civil War Amendments. Hence
they tend to be underva lued or even forgotten by our citi¬≠
zens. It shou ld be the responsibility of the Cour t to teach
other w ise, to remind our people that the Framers considered
str uctura l protecti ons of freedom the most impor tant ones,
for wh ich reason they a lone were embodied in the or ig ina l
Constituti on and not lef t to later amendment. The frag men¬≠
tati on of power produced by the str ucture of our Gover n¬≠
ment is centra l to liber ty, and when we destroy it, we place
liber ty at per il. TodayвАЩs decisi on shou ld have vindicated,
shou ld have taught, th is tr uth; instead, our judg ment today
has disregarded it.
 For the reasons here stated, we wou ld пђБnd the Act inva lid
in its entirety. We respectfu lly dissent.

 Justice Thomas, dissenting.
 I dissent for the reasons stated in our joint opini on, but I
wr ite separately to say a word about the Commerce Clause.
The joint dissent and The Chief Justice correctly apply

<a id="page-190"></a>
### Page 190

708 NATIONAL FEDERATION OF INDEPENDENT
 BUSINESS v. SEBELIUS
 Thomas, J., dissenting

our precedents to conclude that the Individua l Mandate is
beyond the power granted to Congress under the Commerce
Clause and the Necessary and Proper Clause. Under those
precedents, Congress may reg u late вАЬeconomic activity [that]
substantia lly affects interstate commerce. вАЭ Un i ted Sta tes v.
Lo pez, 514 U. S. 549, 560 (1995). I adhere to my view that
вАЬ the very noti on of a вАШsubstantia l effectsвАЩ test under the Com¬≠
merce Clause is inconsistent w ith the or ig ina l understanding
of CongressвАЩ powers and w ith th is Cour tвАЩs ear ly Commerce
Clause cases. вАЭ Un i ted Sta tes v. M o r r iso n, 529 U. S. 598, 627
(2000) (Thomas, J., concurr ing); see a lso Lo pez, supra, at
584 вАУ 602 (same); Go nzales v. Raich, 545 U. S. 1, 67вАУ 69 (2005)
(Thomas, J., dissenting). As I have explained, the Cour tвАЩs
continued use of that test вАЬ has encouraged the Federa l Gov¬≠
er nment to persist in its view that the Commerce Clause has
vir tua lly no limits. вАЭ M o r r iso n, supra, at 627. The Gov¬≠
er nmentвАЩs unprecedented claim in th is suit that it may reg¬≠
u late not only economic activity but a lso in act i v i ty that
substantia lly affects interstate commerce is a case in point.
